margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-19"><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-20" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-21"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-22">4</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-23">q</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-24">ρ</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-25"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-26">π</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-27">d</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-28">μ</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-1"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi mathvariant="italic">R</mi><mi mathvariant="italic">e</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><mi>d</mi><mi>u</mi><mi>ρ</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>μ</mi></mrow></mfrac></mstyle></mrow><mrow><mo>=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><mn>4</mn><mi>q</mi><mi>ρ</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>π</mi><mi>d</mi><mi>μ</mi></mrow></mfrac></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/medium/am4c12929_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. SEM images of (a-d) PBA-1 to PBA-4.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c12929&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">According to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a>, when the fluid and flow rate are determined, the inner diameter of the pipeline where the fluid is located directly affects the flow state of the fluid. The larger the inner diameter, the smaller the <i>Re</i>, and the weaker the turbulence of the fluid. In a microreactor with an inner diameter of 0.8 mm, the reduced fluid flow intensity minimizes disturbances to the crystal growth. However, this also increases the likelihood of crystal agglomeration. Irregular crystal growth and aggregation often lead to inconsistent Fe-PBA performance. Therefore, for this study, a microreactor with a length of 30 cm and an inner diameter of 0.5 mm was selected as the optimal structural configuration.</div></div><div id="sec3_1_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i12">3.1.2.  Effect of Operating Parameters</h4><div class="NLM_p">The SEM images of Fe-PBA prepared under various operating parameters are presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>. A comparison of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a with <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>a-b reveals that when the <b>temperature</b> is reduced to 25 °C, the morphology of the prepared Fe-PBA crystal exhibits a significant improvement. Although there is still noticeable agglomeration, a regular cubic structure is clearly observable. Additionally, the particle size of the sample under this temperature is significantly reduced, leading to a narrower particle size distribution. When the temperature continues to decrease to 10 °C, there is a marked increase in the agglomeration of Fe-PBA crystals, presenting large clusters within the observed field. Concurrently, the size of the individual crystal particles diminishes. This is attributed to the enhanced crystal growth rate at the elevated temperature of 40 °C, where the dominant crystal faces are prone to excessive development, leading to irregularly shaped crystals. This aberrant growth broadens the distribution of crystal particle sizes and potentially compromises the quality of the final product. At the moderate temperature of 25 °C, the reaction kinetics and crystal growth rates within the microreactor are decreased compared to 40 °C, fostering the formation of crystals with more regular and well-defined shapes. At the lower boundary of 10 °C, the sluggish crystal growth rate results in the premature agglomeration of tiny crystal nuclei before they have a chance to fully mature, culminating in the creation of densely packed cluster-like structures. Therefore, a preparation temperature of 25 °C is deemed optimal for the synthesis of Fe-PBA.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/medium/am4c12929_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. SEM images of (a-d) PBA-5 to PBA-10</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c12929&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">From <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a, c, and d, it can be seen that the samples prepared under the three different <b>flow rates</b> exhibit similar morphologies, each prominently displaying a well-defined cubic shape with a narrow particle size distribution. It is evident that with an increase in flow rates, there is a corresponding decrease in the particle size of Fe-PBA, alongside a progressive rise in the level of agglomeration. This is because when the flow rate is high, the mixing uniformity when the fluid comes into contact is not enough, resulting in excessive local concentration of reactants and explosive nucleation, generating a large number of small crystal nuclei that aggregate together. Furthermore, experiments conducted at a flow rate of 0.3 mL/min resulted in the clogging of Fe-PBA within the microreactor pipeline, indicating that at such a low flow rate, the residence time of the product in the microreactor is excessively prolonged. Therefore, a flow rate of 0.5 mL/min has been identified as the optimal feed flow rate for the Fe-PBA preparation.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Inspection of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c, e, and f suggests that Fe-PBA prepared with <b>Na</b><sub><b>4</b></sub><b>Fe(CN)</b><sub><b>6</b></sub> <b>concentration</b> of 0.05 mol/L takes on an irregular blocky morphology, characterized by large particle size and a broad particle size distribution. At a Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> concentration of 0.1 mol/L, the Fe-PBA crystals adopt a more uniform cubic shape with a marked reduction in the particle size. Further increasing the Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> concentration to 0.2 mol/L leads to the Fe-PBA particles aggregating into larger irregular cubes, although the individual crystals remain with a narrow particle size distribution. The underlying reason for these morphological changes is that at low reactant concentrations, the nucleation driving force is diminished, leading to a smaller number of nucleation sites. Under such conditions, the impediment to crystal growth is minimal particularly on favorable growth surfaces where the crystals can extend substantially, resulting in varied forms such as T-shaped and folded. With increasing reactant concentration, both the nucleation driving force and the frequency of nucleation sites rise alongside an augmentation in the resistance to crystal growth. This sequence of events translates to a decrease in crystal size and a progressive uniform in shape. Upon further concentration elevation, a large number of the systems gives rise to an abundance of fine crystals, which are prone to mutual adhesion and subsequent aggregation into larger clusters. Therefore, a concentration of 0.1 mol/L for Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> was identified as the ideal feed concentration for the preparation of Fe-PBA.</div></div><div id="sec3_1_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_3"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h4 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">3.1.3.  Effect of Additive Additions</h4><div class="NLM_p">The SEM images depicting Fe-PBA prepared under various additive addition conditions are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>. A comparison of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a and b reveals that a high dosage of <b>sodium citrate</b> results in crystals with  uniform cubic morphology. Conversely, when the amount of sodium citrate added is reduced to three times the molar equivalent of FeSO<sub>4</sub>, the obtained material lacks defined morphology or discrete crystals with distinct crystal boundaries and a high degree of agglomeration. The average particle size is approximately 1 μm, and there is a considerable spread particle size distribution. At this reduced sodium citrate concentration, issues such as pipeline blockage were encountered during the preparation process. This phenomenon correlates with the role of sodium citrate, which, as depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>, can complex with Fe<sup>2+</sup> to form ferrous citrate, thereby diminishing the free Fe<sup>2+</sup> concentration, stabilizing it against oxidation, and decelerating the reaction rate. Such conditions are conducive to the formation of Fe-PBA with fewer defects. A high amount of sodium citrate ensures ample chelation with Fe<sup>2+</sup>, and the resulting regular morphology indicates that sodium citrate effectively moderates the reaction kinetics and improves Fe-PBA crystallinity. Insufficient sodium citrate leads to inadequate chelation, precipitating an extremely rapid reaction rate. This not only leads to a multitude of vacancy defects and crystal water within the crystal structure but also prompts irregular crystal to growth. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> This quick formation of these aggregates is the primary cause of  pipeline blockage. Taking into account the preparation effect and cost, this study identifies the optimal sodium citrate quantity as 5 times the molar equivalent of FeSO<sub>4</sub>.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/medium/am4c12929_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. SEM images of (a-d) PBA-11 to PBA-14.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c12929&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/medium/am4c12929_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Preparation mechanism diagram of Fe-PBA (a) without adding and (b) adding trisodium citrate, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c12929&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">From <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 4</a>a, c, and d, it is apparent that with a high concentration of <b>ascorbic acid</b> Fe-PBA crystals manifest as well-defined, uniform cubes featuring a low degree of aggregation, small particle size, and narrow particle size distribution. However, when the concentration is reduced to 0.1 times the molar equivalent of FeSO<sub>4</sub>, the Fe-PBA crystals become coarse, predominantly maintaining a cubic shape but with instances of crystals clustering together. This results in an irregular morphology, a broad particle size distribution, and a significant variance in particle size. The reason behind this is that ascorbic acid not only serves as an antioxidant but also forms comparatively weak complexes with Fe<sup>2+</sup>. With a limited addition of ascorbic acid, both the coordination and antioxidant effects are subdued, leading to diminished control over crystal growth and, consequently, a marked disparity in the particle sizes of Fe-PBA products. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26 ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref26 ref35" aria-label="reference 26 and 35">(26,35)</a> Taking into consideration the synthesis quality and cost efficiency, this study has determined that adding ascorbic acid at 0.2 times the molar equivalent of FeSO<sub>4</sub> is the optimal concentration.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, the optimal synthesis conditions for Fe-PBA were established from the data gathered from the 13th sample group. These conditions include a tube length of 30 cm, a tube diameter of 0.5 mm, an operating temperature of 25 °C, and a singular feed flow rate of 0.5 mL/min. Additionally, the feed concentration of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> was set at 0.1 mol/L. To enhance the synthesis process, sodium citrate and ascorbic acid were added in quantities 5 and 0.2 times the molar equivalent of FeSO<sub>4</sub>, respectively. These parameters were selected as the benchmark for the most efficient production of high-quality Fe-PBA crystals.</div></div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i14">3.2.  Material Characterization Results of PBA-13 and PBA-15</h3><div class="NLM_p">As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a, PBA-13 crystallizes into a well-defined cubic morphology with sharply delineated, homogeneously sized particles, predominantly measuring 1.24 μm in diameter. The particle size is relatively substantial and exhibits a narrowly confined distribution. Such uniformity can be attributed to the jet-flow-like reaction environment fostered by the T-shaped collision microreactor, which features a minimal contact surface area and a high mass transfer efficiency, effectively narrowing the gap between mixing rate and reaction rate. This setup swiftly mitigates system supersaturation post nucleation, thereby facilitating robust crystal growth. Moreover, the absence of material backmixing within the microreactor ensures a steady concentration of reactants and a consistent inflow, effectively preventing secondary nucleation while fostering uniform growth up to the micrometer scale. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> In contrast, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b unveils PBA-15 with an amorphous, blocky morphology and a primary particle size of mainly  1.76 μm. The particle size distribution appears markedly broadened, a result of significant backmixing in the beaker where the reactants are rapidly depleted during nucleation prior to achieving uniform mixing. Consequently, the beaker’s internal system fails to maintain a supersaturation level conducive to nucleation. Under vigorous stirring and collision, crystal nuclei tend to clump together into uneven aggregates, a condition decidedly adverse to the electrochemical performance of the material. Furthermore, TEM images in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>c-d showcase PBA-13’s consistent cubic-like structures. The selected region electron diffraction (SAED) pattern observed from a single PBA-13 particle along the crystal axis [100] (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>e) corroborates the single-crystalline nature of the PBA-13 lattice, underscoring its structural integrity.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/medium/am4c12929_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. SEM images and particle size distribution diagrams of materials: (a) PBA-13; (b) PBA-15. TEM images of PBA-1 at (c) 500 nm and (d) 200 nm sizes. (e) SAED pattern of PBA-13.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c12929&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of Fe-PBA is significantly influenced by the concentration of [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4–</sup> vacancy defects and the sodium content within the material’s structure. These critical parameters are directly reflected in the material’s molecular formula, which can be denoted as Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub><i>y</i></sub>□<sub>1–<i>y</i></sub>, where □ represents a [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4–</sup> vacancy defect. To accurately determine the molecular formula of  Fe-PBA, ICP spectroscopy and elemental analysis tests were conducted on PBA-13 and PBA-15. The outcomes of these analyses, alongside the derived molecular formulas, are presented in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table 2</a>. The data elucidate that the PBA-13 crystal harbors fewer [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4–</sup> vacancy defects compared to that in PBA-15, and it also contains a higher sodium content than that in PBA-15. This indicates that the microreactor environment promotes a consistent and controlled growth for Fe-PBA crystals, effectively suppressing the generation of vacancy defects and enhancing the incorporation of sodium ions. This conducive synthetic setting facilitated by the microreactor is pivotal for yielding Fe-PBA materials with superior electrochemical properties.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl2"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 2. ICP, EA and Molecular Formula Calculation Results</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="left"></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">Number</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">Na mass fraction (%)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">Fe mass fraction (%)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">C&nbsp;mass fraction (%)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char=".">N&nbsp;mass fraction (%)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">Molecular formula</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">PBA-13</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">9.91</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">30.18</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">18.76</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">21.54</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">Na<sub>1.54</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.93</sub></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">PBA-15</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">9.83</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">32.92</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">19.58</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">21.38</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">Na<sub>1.35</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.85</sub></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">To determine the crystal structure and water content of the material, XRD, FTIR, and TG tests were conducted on PBA-13 and PBA-15, respectively. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a, the XRD patterns of both Fe-PBA samples align with the standard reference pattern for Prussian blue (JCPDS, No. 52-1907), confirming the absence of extraneous impurities and establishing a cubic crystal structure with the <i>Fm-</i>3̅<i>m</i> space group. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> Notably, PBA-13 is distinguished by its more pronounced characteristic peaks, indicative of its superior crystallinity and the sparser presence of vacancy defects within the crystal lattice. A closer inspection of the 15°–45° range in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a, as presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b, reveals a noteworthy trend: the primary XRD corresponding to the (200), (220), and (400) crystallographic planes the three Fe-PBA sets are observed to shift toward lower angles in contrast to the standard reference. This is due to the incorporation of sodium ions into the crystal, which consequently enlarges the lattice parameters and provokes a distortion of the crystal framework, leading to the observed alteration in the peak positions. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> The higher the sodium content, the more pronounced the peak shift, which is consistent with the calculated results of the molecular formula.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/medium/am4c12929_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0007.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. (a) XRD diagram and (b) partial enlarged diagram of PBA-13 and PBA-15.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c12929&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>a illustrates the TG curves of the two samples, where a pronounced weight reduction is observed in the temperature range  30–290 °C. This mass loss is attributed to the evaporation of water from the samples. Beyond 290 °C, the weight loss can be attributed to the thermal decomposition of the Fe-PBA. The dehydration process from 30 to 290 °C occurs in two distinct stages. In the initial phase, from 30 to 200 °C, the water is associated with the zeolite, which is absorbed on the surface and within the crystal pores. This water is not chemically bonded to the Fe-PBA and is, therefore, readily removed. The second phase, from 200 to 290 °C, involves bound water that is situated at the [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4–</sup> vacancy defects within the material and is covalently bonded to the Fe atoms in the crystal lattice, rendering its removal more challenging. Moreover, the bound water in Fe-PBA is prone to intrusion into the battery during charging and discharging, potentially compromising the battery environment and impinging on the cycling performance of the battery. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> The low content of bound water in the PBA-13 crystal suggests fewer structural defects and a higher crystallinity.</div><figure id="fig8" data-index="8" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 8</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/medium/am4c12929_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0008.jpeg" id="gr8" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 8. (a) TG curves and (b) FTIR spectra of PBA-13 and PBA-15.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c12929&amp;id=fig8"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>b presents the FT-IR spectra for PBA-15, with O–H bond absorption peaks at 3590 and 3620 cm<sup>–1</sup>, and H–O–H bond absorption peaks at 1617 cm<sup>–1</sup>, which are indicative of crystalline water. Conversely, these absorption peaks are diminished in the FTIR spectrum of PBA-13 signaling a lower water content within the PBA-13 crystal. The peaks located at 2060 and 594 cm<sup>–1</sup> are associated with −C≡N and Fe–CN in the Fe-PBA crystal lattice, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The higher absorption peak in PBA-13 at these wavelengths suggests a higher prevalence of corresponding chemical bonds, pointing to a reduced number of [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4–</sup> vacancy defects. The characterization results of TG and FTIR are consistent with the calculated results of the molecular formula.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a> illustrates the XPS spectra of the two samples. In the Fe 2p spectral region of both Fe-PBAs, two distinct doublet peaks are discernible. The first doublet corresponds to the Fe(II), with Fe<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> at 708.3 eV and Fe<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> at 721.1 eV. The second doublet is attributable to Fe(III), with Fe<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub> at 711.3 eV and Fe<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> at 724 eV. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> The relative proportions of these peak areas reflect the iron valence state distribution within Fe-PBA. Upon quantification of the peak areas of Fe<sup>II</sup> and Fe<sup>III</sup> in the XPS spectra of two sets of Fe-PBA samples, the Fe<sup>II</sup>/Fe<sup>III</sup> ratio in PBA-13 is determined to be 1.79, signifying that Fe<sup>II</sup> constitutes 64.2% of the iron present. In contrast, the Fe<sup>II</sup>/Fe<sup>III</sup> ratio in PBA-15 is 1.62, with Fe<sup>II</sup> accounting for 61.8%. These finding suggests that PBA-13 possesses a lower degree of iron oxidation, indicative of a higher sodium content with the crystal lattice exhibiting fewer vacancy defects, enhanced capacity, and greater structural stability.</div><figure id="fig9" data-index="9" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 9</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/medium/am4c12929_0009.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0009.jpeg" id="gr9" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 9. XPS (a) full spectrum, (b) Fe 2p fine spectrum, (c) Na 1s fine spectrum, (d) C 1s fine spectrum, and (e) N 1s fine spectrum of PBA-13 and PBA-15.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c12929&amp;id=fig9"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">The above characterization results indicate that, thanks to the uniform and stable growth environment of the microreactor and the preparation environment that isolates oxygen, the PBA-13 crystal prepared in it has a regular cubic appearance, narrow particle size distribution, high crystallinity, fewer [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4–</sup> vacancy defects, and low oxidation degree and theoretically has better electrochemical performance. Next, two sets of samples will be assembled into button type batteries for electrochemical testing to verify the hypothesis.</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i16" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i16">3.3.  Electrochemical Performances</h3><div class="NLM_p">The rate performance of PBA-13 and PBA-15 was tested at current densities of 0.1–5 C (1 C = 170 mA/g), as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>a. It can be seen that PBA-13 consistently exhibits a superior specific capacity across all tested current densities when compared to PBA-15, and the disparity in specific capacities widens as the current density increases. At a current density of 5 C, the capacity retention rate of PBA-13 can reach 52.8%, which is higher than the 37.2% of PBA-15. This indicates that PBA-13 is capable of sustaining deep charge–discharge cycles even under strenuous conditions, implying that sodium ions are able to penetrate deeper into the crystal structure and that there is a relatively minor impediment to sodium ion transport within its crystal lattice. After experiencing a high current density cycle and then returning to a low current density of 0.1 C, PBA-13 recovers up to 96.8% of its capacity, a marked improvement over PBA-15’s recovery rate of 76.1%. This recovery performance indicates that PBA-13 crystals incur less damage from high-rate cycling, and consequently, the material exhibits enhanced stability.</div><figure id="fig10" data-index="10" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 10</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/medium/am4c12929_0010.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0010.jpeg" id="gr10" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 10. (a) Rate performance diagram of PBA-13 and PBA-15. (b) Discharge curve of PBA-13. (c) Cycle performance and Coulombic efficiency diagram of PBA-13 and PBA-15.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0010.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c12929&amp;id=fig10"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>b shows the charge–discharge curves of PBA-13 over multiple cycles. It can be seen that there are two distinct discharge platforms at around 3.4 and 3 V for a different number of discharge curves, which correspond to the discharge of Fe atoms in disparate chemical environments within the Fe-PBA structure. The higher potential plateau at 3.4 V is attributed to the low-spin Fe atom (Fe<sup>L</sup>), which is bonded to the C atom of the C≡N bond in the Fe-PBA framework, contributing a modest specific capacity ranging from 10 to 20 mAh/g. The lower potential plateau at 3 V is associated with high-spin Fe atoms (Fe<sup>H</sup>) bonded to the N atom of C≡N bond, providing a substantial specific capacity of 70 mAh/g. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Ideally, Fe-PBA crystals should contain equal amounts of Fe<sup>L</sup> and Fe<sup>H</sup> undergoing identical redox reactions and consuming equal charges, which would result in two discharge plateaus of equal duration at their respective potentials. In practice, however, the discharge platform at 3 V is barely noticeable and its contribution to capacity is almost negligible. This is ascribed to the higher redox potential of Fe<sup>L</sup>, which is less readily activated; hence, fewer Fe<sup>L</sup> are effectively involved in the reaction. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> As the number of cycles increases, the potentials of  both discharge platforms gradually decrease, and the discharge platforms themselves become shorter. This phenomenon is attributed to polarization effects occurring during the charging and discharging process of the battery, which leads to structural damage in the material structure, widening the potential gap between the charging and discharging potentials and consequently diminishing the specific capacity. This degradation is a common occurrence in battery operation. It is worth noting that there is an additional discharge platform at 2.75 V in the first discharge curve of the battery. This may be due to the generation of other substances with electrochemical activity under the action of current before the battery starts cycling and stabilizes, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a> but their electrode reaction reversibility is relatively poor, so they will gradually disappear in subsequent cycles and merge into the Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> discharge platform. This is also the reason  the specific capacity of the battery decays rapidly in the early stage of the cycle.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>c shows the comparative cycling performance of PBA-13 and PBA-15 at a current density of 0.5 C. The initial specific capacity of PBA-13 is recorded at 94.1 mAh/g; following 2000 cycles, it diminishes to 68.3 mAh/g, equating to an impressive capacity retention rate of 72.6% that underscores the robust cycling endurance. The initial specific capacity of the material is not high due to its relatively high water content. Water molecules occupy some of the electrochemical active sites, resulting in capacity loss. However, the material position its cyclic performance among the highest in  recent comparable research works (as shown in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl3" href="#tbl3">Table 3</a>). In contrast, PBA-15 starts with an initial specific capacity of 95.0 mAh/g but experiences a precipitous decline to 33.7 mAh/g after 200 cycles, which corresponds to a significantly lower capacity retention rate of 35.4%. The relatively rapid capacity fade of PBA-15 points to a higher prevalence of vacancy defects within its crystal structure and greater susceptibility to structural damage during cycling, leading to reduced stability. This observation aligns with previous molecular formula calculations. In addition, the Coulombic efficiency of PBA-13 remained around 100% throughout the cycles, indicating minimal impurity presence and highly reversible electrode reactions in stark contrast to the lower efficiency exhibited by PBA-15, which hovers around 92%. Notably, the specific capacities of PBA-13 and PBA-15 do not decline linearly, but instead show a sharper reduction during the first 100 cycles ─ 9.8% for PBA-13 and a substantial 56.7% for PBA-15, followed by a more gradual decay. After approximately 600 cycles, PBA-13’s specific capacity decreases by 19.6% and then stabilizes, oscillating around 75 mAh/g. This could be attributed to the presence of other electrochemically active substances within the battery that contributed additional capacity initially, but have poor cycling performance and rapidly degrade within the first 100 cycles. Between 100 to 600 cycles, the specific capacity loss is primarily due to the degradation Fe<sup>L</sup>, with the rate of decay slowing. After 600 cycles, the capacity is predominantly provided by Fe<sup>H</sup>, and the battery reaches a stable phase with negligible further capacity loss, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> corroborating the findings depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>b.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl3"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 3. List of Cyclic Performance of the Representative PBA Materials</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Na<sub>1.70</sub>FeFe(CN)<sub>6</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">200</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">100</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="%">70.5%</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" char=".">200</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="%">73%</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left"> <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref43" aria-label="reference 43">(43)</a></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">Na<sub>1.54</sub>Fe[Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sub>0.93</sub></td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">85</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2000</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char="%">72.6%</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="left">This work</td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p">To further elucidate the phase structural dynamics of PBA materials during the charging and discharging process, In-situ XRD assessments were conducted on PBA-13 at  17 mA/g. The findings, as delineated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 11</a>, reveal that during different stages of the charging and discharging process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 11</a>a), the XRD patterns of PBA-13 consistently exhibit intact Prussian blue analog crystallographic characteristic peaks. This indicates that PBA-13 undergoes minimal phase changes and maintains outstanding stability during charge–discharge cycles, accounting for its excellent cycling performance. In addition, as delineated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 11</a>b, there was a slight splitting of the diffraction peak at the (220) crystal plane due to the high sodium content of the material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> Moreover, as plotted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 11</a>c-e, with the advancement of the charging process, as Na<sup>+</sup> was progressively released, the diffraction peaks at the (200) (220) and (400) crystallographic planes gradually returned to the peak positions of the standard curve, diminishing the splitting at the (220) crystal plane. On the contrary, during the discharge process, the embedding of Na<sup>+</sup> causes lattice distortions and a shift of diffraction peaks toward smaller angles. However, these shifts are relatively minor, all under 0.4°, indicating that the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and extraction exert minimal impact on the material’s structure.</div><figure id="fig11" data-index="11" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 11</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/medium/am4c12929_0011.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0011.jpeg" id="gr11" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 11. (a) Typical charge–discharge profile at a current density of 0.1 C. (b) In-situ XRD patterns of PBA-13 electrode at different charge or discharge states. (c-e) In-situ XRD patterns of (200) (220) and (400) crystallographic planes of PBA-13 electrode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0011.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c12929&amp;id=fig11"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To elucidate the electrode reaction mechanism and internal transformations occurring within Fe-PBA during charging and discharging, cyclic voltammetry (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) tests were conducted on PBA-13. As depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>a,  CV profile PBA-13 is characterized by a high degree of loop closure, signifying a stable charging/discharging process within the chosen voltage window with no evidence of polarization phenomenon in the high voltage region. Notably, the voltammograms feature two pronounced redox peaks at 3.1/2.8 V and 3.5/3.3 V, which correspond to the Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup>electrode reactions for the Fe<sup>H</sup> and Fe<sup>L</sup> in Fe-PBA, respectively. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref44 ref45" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref44 ref45" aria-label="reference 44 and 45">(44,45)</a> The peak polarization values are modest at 0.3 and 0.2 V, indicative of the material’s favorable dynamic characteristics. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> Additionally, the CV trace includes a prominent discharge peak at 2.6 V, attributed to other electrochemically active species emerging within the battery, with a corresponding charge peak potential at 3.1 V. The discharge peak’s intensity diminishes with subsequent  cycles, while the peak at 2.8 V grows stronger, suggesting a decline in cycling stability for the species at 2.6 V as it gradually converts to Fe<sup>2+</sup> and coalesces with the 2.8 V discharge peak. This observation aligns with the cycling performance data. Furthermore, multiple minor charge–discharge peaks between 3.6 and 3.7 V, which may arise from alternate sodium ion extraction/intercalation sites within the Fe-PBA framework, are also discernible during cycling. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref41" aria-label="reference 41">(41)</a></div><figure id="fig12" data-index="12" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 12</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/medium/am4c12929_0012.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0012.jpeg" id="gr12" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 12. (a) Cyclic voltammogram curves of PBA-13. (b) Nyquist diagram of PBA-13 at different cycles. (c) Fitting curve of <i>Z</i><sub>re</sub> and ω<sup>–1/2</sup> of PBA-13 in low frequency region. (d) Equivalent circuit diagram to be used.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c12929/asset/images/large/am4c12929_0012.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c12929&amp;id=fig12"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To interpret the results of the EIS tests we used the equivalent circuit model presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>d, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> with the corresponding fitted outcomes depicted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>a. Within this model, <i>R</i><sub>e</sub> represents the internal resistance encompassing the contact impedance of the electrolyte and electrode of the battery, which corresponds to the <i>Z</i><sub>re</sub>-axis intercept in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>b. <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> signifies the charge transfer impedance at the electrode electrolyte interface, associated with the semicircle in the midto-high frequency region of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>b. <i>W</i><sub>0</sub> and <i>CPE</i><sub>1</sub> represent the Warburg diffusion or sodium ion diffusion process, which correlates with the linear part in the low-frequency region in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>b. The diffusion coefficient <i>D</i> of sodium ion diffusion process can be calculated using the following formula: <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-29"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-30"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-31"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-32"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-33">D</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-34"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-35" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-36"><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-37"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-38" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-39">R</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-40" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-41">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-42"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-43" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-44">T</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-45" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-46">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-47" style="margin-left: 0.111em; margin-right: 0.111em;">/</span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-48"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-49" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">(</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-50">2</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-51"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-52" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-53">A</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-54" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-55">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-56"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-57" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-58">n</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-59" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-60">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-61"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-62" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-63">F</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-64" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-65">4</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-66"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-67" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-68">C</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-69" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-70">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-71"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-72" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-73">σ</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-74" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-75">2</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-76" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">)</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-2"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>D</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msup><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>T</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><mo>/</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mn>2</mn><msup><mrow><mi>A</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>n</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>F</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>C</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><msup><mrow><mi>σ</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div>where <i>R</i> is the gas constant, <i>T</i> is the absolute temperature, <i>A</i> is the surface area of the positive electrode of the battery, <i>n</i> is the number of single molecule transfer charges of the electrode active substance, <i>F</i> is the Faraday constant, and <i>C</i> is the concentration of sodium ions in the electrode. In a determined battery, these quantities are all constant values. σ is the Warburg factor, and it is calculated by the following formula:<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-77"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-78"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-79"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-80"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-81"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-82" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-83">Z</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-84" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-85">re</span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-86"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-87" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-88"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-89"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-90" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-91">R</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-92" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-93">e</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-94" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-95"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-96" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-97">R</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-98" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-99">ct</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-100" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-101">σ</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-102"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-103" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-104">ω</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-105" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-106">−</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-107">1</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-108">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-109">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-3"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>Z</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>re</mi></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi>R</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>ct</mi></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><mi>σ</mi><msup><mrow><mi>ω</mi></mrow><mrow><mo>−</mo><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">According to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eqs 2">eqs 2</a> and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="3">3</a>, σ is negatively correlated with <i>D</i>. Perform linear fitting is performed on <i>Z</i><sub>re</sub> and ω<sup>–1/2</sup> in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 11</a>a, and the fitting result is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>c. The slope of the obtained line is σ. The values of <i>R</i><sub>e</sub>, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and calculated <i>D</i> obtained from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a> are shown in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl4" href="#tbl4">Table 4</a>.</div><div class="NLM_table-wrap" id="tbl4"><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption"><div class="NLM_caption" id=""><div class="title2">Table 4. Electrode Impedance Parameters and σ Value of PBA-1 under Different Cycles</div></div></div><div class="scrollable-table-wrap" style="display: block;"><table class="table "><colgroup><col align="left"><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."><col align="char" char="."></colgroup><thead><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center">Cycle number</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>R</i><sub>e</sub> (Ω)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> (Ω)</th><th class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="col" align="center" char="."><i>D</i></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">1</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">33.01</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2759</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.22&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>–15</sup></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">5</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">32.55</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2425</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.62&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>–15</sup></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">10</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">32.02</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2117</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">1.88&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>–15</sup></td></tr><tr valign="top" class="colsep0"><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" scope="row" align="left">30</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">31.19</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2054</td><td class="colsep0 rowsep0" align="char" char=".">2.22&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>–15</sup></td></tr></tbody></table></div></div><div class="NLM_p last">Observations from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>b reveal that as the cycle number increases, the semicircle in the midto-high frequency region gradually decreases, with only slight variance discernible between the 10th and 30th cycles. Based on the impedance data in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl4" href="#tbl4">Table 4</a>, it can be seen that during the cycling process the solution impedance <i>R</i><sub>e</sub> undergoes minimal alteration, while the charge transfer impedance <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> significantly decreases and gradually stabilizes after the 10th cycle. This trend can be attributed to the electrode–electrolyte interface reaching an equilibrium state after the 10th cycle, which then remains stable in the subsequent cycles elucidating the outstanding cycling stability of PBA-13. The fitted <i>D</i> value reflecting the diffusion coefficient progressively increases, with the rate of change markedly tapering after the 10th cycle. This suggests that the cycling process gradually activates the material, and after the 10th cycle, the material is optimally leading to the formation of a stable interface within the battery. Such findings underscore the battery’s rapid activation capability and robust long-term performance.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i21">4.  Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, this study successfully synthesized Fe-PBA using an innovative T-shaped collision microreactor and explored the influence of the reactor’s design and operational parameters on the resultant material. The optimal apparatus and process conditions were determined to be a reactor tube length of 30 cm, with an internal diameter of 0.5 mm, an operating temperature of 25 °C, a single feed flow rate of 0.5 mL/min, a feed concentration of Na<sub>4</sub>Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub> at 0.1 mol/L, and the addition of sodium citrate and ascorbic acid in molar ratios of 5:1 and 1:5 to FeSO<sub>4</sub>, respectively. Characterization results indicate that  synthesized Fe-PBA boasts a uniform morphology, a narrow particle size distribution, and a notable reduction in [Fe(CN)<sub>6</sub>]<sup>4–</sup> vacancy defects in the crystal, leading to a low degree of oxidation. This indicates that the excellent mass transfer effect in the microreactor narrows the gap between the mixing rate and reaction rate, establishing homogeneous and stable crystallization conditions for the material, thereby enabling meticulous regulation of the Fe-PBA growth. The battery employing this material as a cathode exhibited a specific capacity of 94.1 mAh/g at a current density of 0.5 C and maintained a capacity retention rate of 72.6% after 2000 cycles, showcasing exceptional cycle durability. In-situ XRD results demonstrate that the material structure remains essentially stable during the charging and discharging process, exhibiting excellent structural reversibility. EIS results reveal that the battery can form a stable interface that can be formed inside the battery after 10 cycles achieving rapid activation and steady performance. This research not only advances the continuous fabrication of low-defect and high-performance Fe-PBA materials but also suggests the applicability of the microreactor strategy for synthesizing materials with rapid core reaction rates in liquid-phase systems.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="_suppInfo"></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.4c12929" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="authorInformationSection"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="authorInformationSection">Author Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><ul><li><span class="author-information-subsection-header">Corresponding Authors</span><ul><li><div class="authorItemInformation"><span class="hlFld-ContribAuthor strong">Yang Jin</span> - <span class="hlFld-Affiliation affiliation">Engineering Research Center

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  With the unprecedentedly increasing demand for renewable and clean energy sources, the sodium-ion battery (SIB) is emerging as an alternative or complementary energy storage candidate to the present com. lithium-ion battery due to the abundance and low cost of sodium resources.  Layered transition metal oxides and Prussian blue analogs are reviewed in terms of their com. potential as cathode materials for SIBs.  The recent progress in research on their half cells and full cells for the ultimate application in SIBs are summarized.  In addn., their electrochem. performance, suitability for scaling up, cost, and environmental concerns are compared in detail with a brief outlook on future prospects.  It is anticipated that this review will inspire further development of layered transition metal oxides and Prussian blue analogs for SIBs, esp. for their emerging commercialization.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C3EE44004D"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">You, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.-L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yin, Y.-X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Guo, Y.-G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">High-Quality Prussian Blue Crystals as Superior Cathode Materials for Room-Temperature Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Energy Environ.                         cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrolyte design strategies and research progress for room-temperature sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Che, Haiying; Chen, Suli; Xie, Yingying; Wang, Hong; Amine, Khalil; Liao, Xiao-Zhen; Ma, Zi-Feng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Electrolyte design or functional development is very effective at promoting the performance of sodium-ion batteries, which are attractive for electrochem. energy storage devices due to abundant sodium resources and low cost.  This review discusses recent advances on electrolytes for sodium-ion batteries and comprehensive electrolyte design strategies for various materials systems as well as functional applications.  The discussion is divided into three electrolyte types: liq., solid state, and gel state.  Liq. electrolytes are further divided into different solvent types, including org. carbonate ester, ether, ionic liq., and water.  Solid-state electrolytes also contain two types: solid polymer and glass-ceramic composite.  The challenges and prospects of electrolytes for sodium-ion batteries are discussed as well.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1021/acsenergylett.1c01868"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gabriel, E.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Russell, J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  As a promising alternative to the market-leading lithium-ion batteries, low-cost sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are attractive for applications such as large-scale elec. energy storage systems.  The energy d., cycling life, and rate performance of SIBs are fundamentally dependent on dynamic physiochem. reactions, structural change, and morphol. evolution.  Therefore, it is essential to holistically understand SIBs reaction processes, degrdn. mechanisms, and thermal/mech. behaviors in complex working environments.  The recent developments of advanced in situ and operando characterization enable the establishment of the structure- processing- property- performance relationship in SIBs under operating conditions.  This Review summarizes significant recent progress in SIBs exploiting in situ and operando techniques based on X-ray and electron analyses at different time and length scales.  Through the combination of spectroscopy, imaging, and diffraction, local and global changes in SIBs can be elucidated for improving materials design.  The fundamental principles and state-of-the-art capabilities of different techniques are presented, followed by elaborative discussions of major challenges and perspectives.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref9" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1039/D3CC01548C"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Huang, J.                         cas_cit12R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">12</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Direct observation of alternating octahedral and prismatic sodium layers in O3-type transition metal oxides</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Jae Chul; Kwon, Deok-Hwang; Yang, Julia H.; Kim, Hyunchul; Bo, Shou-Hang; Wu, Lijun; Kim, Haegyeom; Seo, Dong-Hwa; Shi, Tan; Wang, Jingyang; Zhu, Yimei; Ceder, Gerbrand</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The oxygen stacking of O3-type layered sodium transition metal oxides (O3-NaTMO2) changes dynamically upon topotactic Na extn. and reinsertion.  While the phase transition from octahedral to prismatic Na coordination that occurs at intermediate desodiation by transition metal slab gliding is well understood, the structural evolution at high desodiation, crucial to achieve high reversible capacity, remains mostly uncharted.  In this work, the phase transitions of O3-type layered NaTMO2 at high voltage are investigated by combining exptl. and computational approaches.  An OP2-type phase that consists of alternating octahedral and prismatic Na layers is directly obsd. by in situ X-ray diffraction and high-resoln. scanning transmission electron microscopy.  The origin of this peculiar phase is explained by at. interactions involving Jahn-Teller active Fe4+ and distortion tolerant Ti4+ that stabilize the local Na environment.  The path-dependent desodiation and resodiation pathways are also rationalized in this material through the different kinetics of the prismatic and octahedral layers, presenting a comprehensive picture about the structural stability of the layered materials upon Na intercalation.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref13"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref13" class="refNumLink">13</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit13" data-doi="10.1039/C9TA08991H"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tripathi, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rudola, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gajjela, S.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synthesis and electrochemical performances of Na3V2(PO4)2F3/C composites as cathode materials for sodium ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Mingxue; Huang, Xiaobing; Wang, Haiyan; Zhou, Tao; Xie, Huasheng; Ren, Yurong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">RSC Advances</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">53</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">30628-30636</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">RSCACL</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na3V2(PO4)2F3 (NVPF) with NASCION (Na superionic conductor) is recognized as a potential cathode material owing to its high theor. capacity.  However, the electronic cond. of NVPF is much lower than its ionic cond., which seriously affects the properties of this material.  The carbon layer can be used as the conductive medium to enhance the cond. of NVPF.  In this study, we propose a single-step solid-state reaction method based on mech. activation with pitch as the carbon source to synthesize NVPF/C composites.  The crystallog. structure and morphol. of all as-prepd. samples were investigated by XRD, Raman spectroscopy, BET measurement, thermal anal., SEM and TEM.  Furthermore, the electrochem. performance and kinetic properties were analyzed by CV, galvanostatic charge-discharge and EIS tests.  These tests outcomes demonstrated that the NVPF/C-2 composite with a carbon content of 12.14 wt% showed an excellent rate performance and cycle stability.  It presented reversible capacities of 103 and 95 mA h g-1 at 0.2 and 10C, resp., and an outstanding retention of 91.9% after 500 cycles at 5C.  These excellent properties of the NVPF/C-2 composite are attributed to its high ion diffusion coeff. and small charge transfer impedance.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref15" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1149/1.1523691"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Barker, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Saidi, M. Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Swoyer, J. L.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">A Sodium-Ion Cell Based on the Fluorophosphate Compound NaVPO<sub>4</sub>F</span>. <i>Electrochem. Solid-State Lett.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2003</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>6</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">1</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">A1</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">A4</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1149/1.1523691</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D6%26publication_year%3D2003%26pages%3DA1-A4%26journal%3DElectrochem.%2BSolid-State%2BLett.%26issue%3D1%26author%3DJ.%2BBarker%26author%3DM.%2BY.%2BSaidi%26author%3DJ.%2BL.%2BSwoyer%26title%3DA%2BSodium-Ion%2BCell%2BBased%2Bon%2Bthe%2BFluorophosphate%2BCompound%2BNaVPO4F%26doi%3D10.1149%252F1.1523691&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.4c12929&amp;doiOfLink=10.1149%2F1.1523691&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A sodium-ion cell based on the fluorophosphate compound NaVPO4F</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Barker, J.; Saidi, M. Y.; Swoyer, J. L.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Electrochemical and Solid-State Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2003</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">6</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">1</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">A1-A4</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ESLEF6</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1099-0062</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Preliminary performance data is presented for a novel sodium-ion cell based on a hard carbon/NaVPO4F chem.  To our knowledge this is the first time that a viable sodium-ion system based on a polyanion cathode material has been demonstrated.  The NaVPO4F was prepd. by a simple incorporation reaction involving vanadium(III) phosphate, VPO4 and NaF.  The hard carbon was from a com. source.  Const. current cycling indicated reversible alkali-ion insertion behavior for the fluorophosphate material, while the generally sym. nature of the charge/discharge curves indicated the energetic reversibility of the insertion system.  The av. discharge voltage for the sodium-ion cells was demonstrated to around 3.7 V, a figure comparable with com. available lithium-ion cells.  The reversible specific capacities for the cathode and anode active materials were detd. to be 82 and 202 mAh/g, resp.  Representative test cells cycled more than 30 times before their discharge capacity had declined to less then 50% of the original.  The relatively high first cycle charge inefficiency is thought to be due to irreversible passivation reactions occurring at the carbon surface.  Significant performance enhancements may be made by optimization of the electrolyte system and improving the active material mass balance.  In summary, low overvoltage, good insertion reversibility, and reasonable capacity retention characterize this fluorophosphate-based sodium-ion cell.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref16" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1016/j.jssc.2019.06.027"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gond, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meena, S.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Highly Crystallized Na2CoFe(CN)6 with Suppressed Lattice Defects as Superior Cathode Material for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wu, Xianyong; Wu, Chenghao; Wei, Congxiao; Hu, Ling; Qian, Jiangfeng; Cao, Yuliang; Ai, Xinping; Wang, Jiulin; Yang, Hanxi</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
